<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png" class="image"><img alt="2006 AEGold Proof Obv.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png/16px-2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png/24px-2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png/32px-2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png 2x" data-file-width="1770" data-file-height="1762" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Libertarianism" title="Portal:Libertarianism">Libertarianism&#32;portal</a></li>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:255px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg/253px-Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg.png" decoding="async" width="253" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg/380px-Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg/506px-Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Political_Spectrum_Chart_NPOV.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two-axis political spectrum chart with an economic axis and a socio-cultural axis, and ideologically representative colors</div></div></div>
<p><b>Individualism</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Moral" title="Moral">moral</a> stance, <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a>, ideology, or social outlook that emphasizes the moral worth of the <a href="/wiki/Individual" title="Individual">individual</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>  Individualists promote the exercise of one's goals and desires and so value <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a> and self-reliance<sup id="cite_ref-thefreedictionary.com_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thefreedictionary.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and advocate that interests of the individual should achieve precedence over the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> or a social group,<sup id="cite_ref-thefreedictionary.com_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thefreedictionary.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> while opposing external interference upon one's own interests by <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a> or <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a> such as the government.<sup id="cite_ref-thefreedictionary.com_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thefreedictionary.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Individualism is often defined in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collectivism" title="Collectivism">collectivism</a>, and more <a href="/wiki/Corporatism" title="Corporatism">corporate</a> social forms.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hayek_1994_17,_37–48_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayek_1994_17,_37–48-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Individualism makes the individual its focus<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and so starts "with the fundamental premise that the human individual is of primary importance in the struggle for liberation."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">Anarchism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> are examples of movements that take the human individual as a central unit of analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Individualism thus involves "the right of the individual to freedom and self-realization".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It has also been used as a term denoting "The quality of being an individual; individuality"<sup id="cite_ref-thefreedictionary.com_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thefreedictionary.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> related to possessing "An individual characteristic; a <a href="/wiki/Idiosyncrasy" title="Idiosyncrasy">quirk</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-thefreedictionary.com_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thefreedictionary.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Individualism is thus also associated with <a href="/wiki/Artistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Artistic">artistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bohemianism" title="Bohemianism">bohemian</a> interests and lifestyles where there is a tendency towards self-creation and experimentation as opposed to tradition or popular mass opinions and behaviors,<sup id="cite_ref-thefreedictionary.com_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thefreedictionary.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bohemia:_The_Underworld_of_Art_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohemia:_The_Underworld_of_Art-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> as with <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanist</a> philosophical positions and ethics.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>, the word "individualism" was first introduced, as a pejorative, by the <a href="/wiki/Owenism" title="Owenism">Owenites</a> in the late 1830s, although it is unclear if they were influenced by <a href="/wiki/Saint-Simonianism" title="Saint-Simonianism">Saint-Simonianism</a> or came up with it independently.<sup id="cite_ref-Claeys_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claeys-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A more positive use of the term in Britain came to be used with the writings of <a href="/wiki/James_Elishama_Smith" title="James Elishama Smith">James Elishama Smith</a>, who was a <a href="/wiki/Millenarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Millenarian">millenarian</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Christian_Israelite" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Israelite">Christian Israelite</a>. Although an early <a href="/wiki/Robert_Owen" title="Robert Owen">Owenite</a> socialist, he eventually rejected its collective idea of property, and found in individualism a "universalism" that allowed for the development of the "original genius." Without individualism, Smith argued, individuals cannot amass property to increase one's happiness.<sup id="cite_ref-Claeys_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claeys-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Maccall" title="William Maccall">William Maccall</a>, another <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> preacher, and probably an acquaintance of Smith, came somewhat later, although influenced by <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">German Romanticism</a>, to the same positive conclusions, in his 1847 work "Elements of Individualism".<sup id="cite_ref-Swart_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swart-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual">Individual</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Individual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Individual" title="Individual">Individual</a></div>
<p>An individual is a person or any specific object in a collection. In the 15th century and earlier, and also today within the fields of <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, individual means "indivisible", typically describing any numerically singular thing, but sometimes meaning "a person." (q.v. "The problem of <a href="/wiki/Proper_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Proper name">proper names</a>"). From the 17th century on, individual indicates separateness, as in individualism.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Individuality is the state or quality of being an individuated being; a person separated from everything with unique character by possessing his or her own needs, goals, and desires in comparison to other persons.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Individuation_principle">Individuation principle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Individuation principle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Individuation" title="Individuation">Individuation</a></div>
<p>The principle of individuation, or <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">principium individuationis</i></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Reese_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reese-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> describes the manner in which a thing is identified as distinguished from other things.<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a>, individuation is a process of transformation, whereby the personal and <a href="/wiki/Collective_unconscious" title="Collective unconscious">collective unconscious</a> is brought into consciousness (by means of dreams, <a href="/wiki/Active_imagination" title="Active imagination">active imagination</a> or <a href="/wiki/Free_association_(psychology)" title="Free association (psychology)">free association</a> to take examples) to be assimilated into the whole personality. It is a completely natural process necessary for the integration of the psyche to take place.<sup id="cite_ref-jung1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jung1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Jung considered individuation to be the central process of human development.<sup id="cite_ref-Jung&#39;s_Individuation_process_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jung&#39;s_Individuation_process-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In <i>L'individuation psychique et collective</i>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Simondon" title="Gilbert Simondon">Gilbert Simondon</a> developed a theory of individual and collective individuation in which the individual subject is considered as an effect of individuation rather than a cause. Thus, the individual atom is replaced by a never-ending <a href="/wiki/Process_philosophy" title="Process philosophy">ontological process</a> of individuation. Individuation is an always incomplete process, always leaving a "pre-individual" left-over, itself making possible future individuations.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Stiegler" title="Bernard Stiegler">Bernard Stiegler</a> draws upon and modifies the work of <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Simondon" title="Gilbert Simondon">Gilbert Simondon</a> on individuation and also upon similar ideas in <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>. For Stiegler "the <i>I</i>, as a psychic individual, can only be thought in relationship to <i>we</i>, which is a collective individual. The <i>I</i> is constituted in adopting a collective tradition, which it inherits and in which a plurality of <i>I</i>&#160;'s acknowledge each other's existence."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Individualism_and_society">Individualism and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Individualism and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Individualism holds that a person taking part in society attempts to learn and discover what his or her own interests are on a personal basis, without a presumed following of the interests of a societal structure (an individualist need not be an <a href="/wiki/Egotism" title="Egotism">egoist</a>). The individualist does not follow one particular philosophy, rather creates an amalgamation of elements of many, based on personal interests in particular aspects that he/she finds of use. On a societal level, the individualist participates on a personally structured political and moral ground. Independent thinking and opinion is a common trait of an individualist. <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, claims that his concept of "<a href="/wiki/General_will" title="General will">general will</a>" in the "<a href="/wiki/Social_Contract_(Rousseau)" class="mw-redirect" title="Social Contract (Rousseau)">social contract</a>" is not the simple collection of individual wills and that it furthers the interests of the individual (the constraint of law itself would be beneficial for the individual, as the lack of respect for the law necessarily entails, in Rousseau's eyes, a form of <a href="/wiki/Ignorance" title="Ignorance">ignorance</a> and submission to one's <a href="/wiki/Passion_(emotion)" title="Passion (emotion)">passions</a> instead of the preferred <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/autonomy" class="extiw" title="wikt:autonomy">autonomy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>).
</p><p>Societies and groups can differ in the extent to which they are based upon predominantly "self-regarding" (individualistic, and/or self-interested) behaviors, rather than "other-regarding" (group-oriented, and group, or society-minded) behaviors. <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Benedict" title="Ruth Benedict">Ruth Benedict</a> made a distinction, relevant in this context, between <a href="/wiki/Guilt_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Guilt society">"guilt" societies</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a>) with an "internal reference standard", and <a href="/wiki/Shame_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Shame society">"shame" societies</a> (e.g., Japan, "bringing shame upon one's ancestors") with an "external reference standard", where people look to their peers for feedback on whether an action is "acceptable" or not.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Individualism is often contrasted<sup id="cite_ref-Hayek_1994_17,_37–48_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayek_1994_17,_37–48-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> either with <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a> or with <a href="/wiki/Collectivism" title="Collectivism">collectivism</a>, but in fact, there is a spectrum of behaviors at the societal level ranging from highly individualistic societies through mixed societies to collectivist.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodological_individualism">Methodological individualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Methodological individualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methodological_individualism" title="Methodological individualism">Methodological individualism</a> is the view that phenomena can only be understood by examining how they result from the motivations and actions of individual agents.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In economics, people's behavior is explained in terms of rational choices, as constrained by prices and incomes. The economist accepts individuals' preferences as givens. <a href="/wiki/Gary_Becker" title="Gary Becker">Becker</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Stigler" title="George Stigler">Stigler</a> provide a forceful statement of this view:</p><blockquote><p>On the traditional view, an explanation of economic phenomena that reaches a difference in tastes between people or times is the terminus of the argument: the problem is abandoned at this point to whoever studies and explains tastes (psychologists? anthropologists? phrenologists? sociobiologists?). On our preferred interpretation, one never reaches this impasse: the economist continues to search for differences in prices or incomes to explain any differences or changes in behavior.<i><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></i></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitive_individualism">Competitive individualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Competitive individualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Competitive individualism is a form of individualism that arises from competitive systems. The function of the system is to maintain an inequality in the society and fields of human engagement. This pins the ups and downs of a person's life onto themselves by not acknowledging a range of factors like influence of socioeconomic class, race, gender, etc. It supports the privilege theories that affirms position of certain individuals higher in the hierarchy of ranks at the expense of others. For better individuality cooperation is considered to be a better remedy for personal growth.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_individualism">Political individualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Political individualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">With the abolition of private property, then, we shall have true, beautiful, healthy Individualism. Nobody will waste his life in accumulating things, and the symbols for things. One will live. To live is the rarest thing in the world. Most people exist, that is all.</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Soul_of_Man_under_Socialism" class="mw-redirect" title="The Soul of Man under Socialism">The Soul of Man under Socialism</a></i>, 1891</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Individualists are chiefly concerned with protecting individual autonomy against obligations imposed by social institutions (such as the state or religious morality). For <a href="/wiki/L._Susan_Brown" title="L. Susan Brown">L. Susan Brown</a>, "Liberalism and anarchism are two political philosophies that are fundamentally concerned with individual <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_will" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom of will">freedom</a> yet differ from one another in very distinct ways. Anarchism shares with liberalism a radical commitment to individual freedom while rejecting liberalism's competitive property relations."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Civil_libertarianism" title="Civil libertarianism">Civil libertarianism</a> is a strain of political thought that supports civil liberties, or which emphasizes the supremacy of <a href="/wiki/Individual_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Individual rights">individual rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Personal_freedom" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal freedom">personal freedoms</a> over and against any kind of authority (such as a <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporation</a>, social norms imposed through <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">peer pressure</a>, etc.).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Civil libertarianism is not a complete <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a>; rather, it is a collection of views on the specific issues of <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a>. Because of this, a civil libertarian outlook is compatible with many other political philosophies, and civil libertarianism is found on both the <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right</a> and <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left</a> in modern politics.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> For scholar <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Meiksins_Wood" title="Ellen Meiksins Wood">Ellen Meiksins Wood</a>, "there are doctrines of individualism that are opposed to <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Lockean individualism</a> ... and non-lockean individualism may encompass <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>British Historians Emily Robinson, Camilla Schofield, Florence Sutcliffe-Braithwaite, and Natalie Thomlinson have argued that by the 1970s Britons were keen about defining and claiming their individual rights, identities and perspectives.  They demanded greater personal autonomy and self-determination and less outside control. They angrily complained that the 'establishment' was withholding it. They argue this shift in concerns helped cause <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>, and was incorporated into Thatcherism's appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberalism">Liberalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Liberalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/List_of_liberal_theorists" title="List of liberal theorists">People</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">

<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_centrism_in_Sweden" title="Liberalism and centrism in Sweden">Sweden</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_radicalism_in_Switzerland" title="Liberalism and radicalism in Switzerland">Switzerland</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Taiwan" title="Liberalism in Taiwan">Taiwan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Thailand" title="Liberalism in Thailand">Thailand</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Tunisia" title="Liberalism in Tunisia">Tunisia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Turkey" title="Liberalism in Turkey">Turkey</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Ukraine" title="Liberalism in Ukraine">Ukraine</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Liberalism in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">United States</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_in_the_United_States" title="Libertarianism in the United States">Classical</a></li>
