<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_socialism" title="Libertarian socialism">Libertarian socialism</a></div>
<p>Libertarian socialism, sometimes dubbed socialist libertarianism,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Left-libertarianism" title="Left-libertarianism">left-libertarianism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> is a group of <a href="/wiki/Anti-authoritarianism" title="Anti-authoritarianism">anti-authoritarian</a><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> political philosophies inside the <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> movement that rejects socialism as centralized state ownership and control of the economy,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> as well as the state itself.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> It criticizes <a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">wage labour relationships within the workplace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>  Instead, it emphasizes <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_self-management" title="Workers&#39; self-management">workers' self-management</a> of the workplace<sup id="cite_ref-:0_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Decentralization#Libertarian_socialist_decentralization" title="Decentralization">decentralized structures of political organization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> It asserts that a society based on freedom and justice can be achieved through abolishing <a href="/wiki/Authoritarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> institutions that control certain <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a> and subordinate the majority to an owning class or political and economic <a href="/wiki/Elite" title="Elite">elite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Libertarian socialists advocate for <a href="/wiki/Decentralization#Libertarian_socialist_decentralization" title="Decentralization">decentralized</a> structures based on <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federalism#Federalism_as_the_anarchist_and_libertarian_socialist_mode_of_political_organization" title="Federalism">federal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederal</a> associations such as <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_municipalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarian municipalism">libertarian municipalism</a>, citizens' assemblies, <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_council" title="Workers&#39; council">workers' councils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> All of this is generally done within a general call for <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">libertarian</a><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Free_association_(communism_and_anarchism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free association (communism and anarchism)">voluntary human relationships</a><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> through the identification, criticism, and practical dismantling of illegitimate authority in all aspects of human life.<sup id="cite_ref-iaf-ifa.org_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaf-ifa.org-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ward_1966_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_1966-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_2002_106_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_2002_106-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> As such libertarian socialism, within the larger socialist movement, seeks to distinguish itself both from <a href="/wiki/Leninism" title="Leninism">Leninism</a>/<a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolshevism</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Past and present political philosophies and movements commonly described as libertarian socialist include <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Anarchist_communism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarchist communism">anarchist communism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collectivist_anarchism" title="Collectivist anarchism">anarchist collectivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalism" title="Anarcho-syndicalism">anarcho-syndicalism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(economic_theory)" title="Mutualism (economic theory)">mutualism</a><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Autonomism" title="Autonomism">autonomism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communalism" title="Communalism">communalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Participism" title="Participism">participism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guild_socialism" title="Guild socialism">guild socialism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_syndicalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutionary syndicalism">revolutionary syndicalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarian Marxist">libertarian Marxist</a><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> philosophies such as <a href="/wiki/Council_communism" title="Council communism">council communism</a><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Luxemburgism" class="mw-redirect" title="Luxemburgism">Luxemburgism</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Graham-2005_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-2005-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> as well as some versions of "<a href="/wiki/Utopian_socialism" title="Utopian socialism">utopian socialism</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Individualist_anarchism" title="Individualist anarchism">individualist anarchism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Left-libertarianism">Left-libertarianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Left-libertarianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Left-libertarianism" title="Left-libertarianism">Left-libertarianism</a></div>
<p>Left-libertarianism, or left-wing libertarianism,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> names several related but distinct approaches to <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, society, culture, and political and social theory, which stress both <a href="/wiki/Political_freedom" title="Political freedom">individual freedom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>.  Unlike <a href="/wiki/Right-libertarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Right-libertarian">right-libertarians</a>, they believe that neither claiming nor <a href="/wiki/Labor_theory_of_property" title="Labor theory of property">mixing one's labor</a> with <a href="/wiki/Natural_resources" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural resources">natural resources</a> is enough to generate full <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a> rights,<sup id="cite_ref-WhyNotIncoherent_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WhyNotIncoherent-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-encyclolib_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encyclolib-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> and maintain that natural resources (land, oil, gold, trees) ought to be held in some <a href="/wiki/Egalitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Egalitarian">egalitarian</a> manner, either unowned or <a href="/wiki/Owned_collectively" class="mw-redirect" title="Owned collectively">owned collectively</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-encyclolib_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encyclolib-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>  Those left-libertarians who support private property do so under the condition that recompense is offered to the local community.
</p><p>Left-libertarianism can refer generally to these related and overlapping schools of thought:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Anti-authoritarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-authoritarian">Anti-authoritarian</a> varieties of left-wing politics and, in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> movement, usually known as <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_socialism" title="Libertarian socialism">libertarian socialism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bookchinreader_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookchinreader-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Geolibertarianism" title="Geolibertarianism">Geolibertarianism</a>: a synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarianism</a> and geoism (or <a href="/wiki/Georgism" title="Georgism">Georgism</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-progress.org_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-progress.org-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hillel_Steiner" title="Hillel Steiner">Steiner</a>–<a href="/wiki/Peter_Vallentyne" title="Peter Vallentyne">Vallentyne</a> school, whose proponents draw conclusions from classical liberal or market liberal premises.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Left-wing_market_anarchism" title="Left-wing market anarchism">Left-wing market anarchism</a>, which stresses the socially transformative potential of non-aggression and <a href="/wiki/Anticapitalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anticapitalist">anticapitalist</a>, <i>freed</i> markets.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right-libertarianism">Right-libertarianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Right-libertarianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right-libertarianism" title="Right-libertarianism">Right-libertarianism</a></div>
<p>Right-libertarianism, or right-wing libertarianism, is a phrase used by some to describe either non-<a href="/wiki/Collectivist" class="mw-redirect" title="Collectivist">collectivist</a> forms of libertarianism<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> or a variety of different libertarian views some label "right" of mainstream libertarianism including "<a href="/wiki/Libertarian_conservatism" title="Libertarian conservatism">libertarian conservatism</a>".
</p><p>The <i>Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i> calls it "right libertarianism", but states: "Libertarianism is often thought of as 'right-wing' doctrine. This, however, is mistaken for at least two reasons. First, on social—rather than economic—issues, libertarianism tends to be 'left-wing'. It opposes laws that restrict consensual and private sexual relationships between adults (e.g., gay sex, non-marital sex, and deviant sex), laws that restrict drug use, laws that impose religious views or practices on individuals, and compulsory military service. Second, in addition to the better-known version of libertarianism—right-libertarianism—there is also a version known as '<a href="/wiki/Left-libertarianism" title="Left-libertarianism">left-libertarianism</a>'. Both endorse full self-ownership, but they differ with respect to the powers agents have to appropriate unappropriated natural resources (land, air, water, etc.)."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_creative_independent_lifestyle">As creative independent lifestyle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: As creative independent lifestyle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg/150px-Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg/225px-Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg/300px-Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2190" data-file-height="3291" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oscar_Wilde_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>, famous <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> writer of the <a href="/wiki/Decadent_movement" title="Decadent movement">decadent movement</a> and famous <a href="/wiki/Dandy" title="Dandy">dandy</a></div></div></div>
<p>The anarchist<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> writer and <a href="/wiki/Bohemianism" title="Bohemianism">bohemian</a> <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a> wrote in his famous essay <i><a href="/wiki/The_Soul_of_Man_under_Socialism" class="mw-redirect" title="The Soul of Man under Socialism">The Soul of Man under Socialism</a></i> that "Art is individualism, and individualism is a disturbing and disintegrating force. There lies its immense value. For what it seeks is to disturb monotony of type, slavery of custom, tyranny of habit, and the reduction of man to the level of a machine."<sup id="cite_ref-flag.blackened.net_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flag.blackened.net-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> For anarchist historian <a href="/wiki/George_Woodcock" title="George Woodcock">George Woodcock</a> "Wilde's aim in <i>The Soul of Man under Socialism</i> is to seek the society most favorable to the artist...for Wilde art is the supreme end, containing within itself enlightenment and regeneration, to which all else in society must be subordinated...Wilde represents the anarchist as <a href="/wiki/Aesthete" class="mw-redirect" title="Aesthete">aesthete</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Anarchism_1962._p._447_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anarchism_1962._p._447-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The word individualism in this way has been used to denote a personality with a strong tendency towards self-creation and experimentation as opposed to tradition or popular mass opinions and behaviors.<sup id="cite_ref-thefreedictionary.com_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thefreedictionary.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bohemia:_The_Underworld_of_Art_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohemia:_The_Underworld_of_Art-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Anarchist writer <a href="/wiki/Murray_Bookchin" title="Murray Bookchin">Murray Bookchin</a> describes a lot of individualist anarchists as people who "expressed their opposition in uniquely personal forms, especially in fiery tracts, outrageous behavior, and aberrant lifestyles in the cultural ghettos of fin de siècle New York, Paris, and London. As a credo, individualist anarchism remained largely a <a href="/wiki/Bohemian" title="Bohemian">bohemian</a> lifestyle, most conspicuous in its demands for sexual freedom ('<a href="/wiki/Free_love" title="Free love">free love</a>') and enamored of innovations in art, behavior, and clothing."<sup id="cite_ref-bohemian_individualism_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bohemian_individualism-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In relation to this view of individuality, French individualist anarchist <a href="/wiki/Emile_Armand" class="mw-redirect" title="Emile Armand">Emile Armand</a> advocates <a href="/wiki/Ethical_egoism" title="Ethical egoism">egoistical</a> denial of social conventions and dogmas to live in accord to one's own ways and desires in daily life since he emphasized anarchism as a way of life and practice. In this way he opines "So the anarchist individualist tends to reproduce himself, to perpetuate his spirit in other individuals who will share his views and who will make it possible for a state of affairs to be established from which authoritarianism has been banished. It is this desire, this will, not only to live, but also to reproduce oneself, which we shall call "activity".<sup id="cite_ref-lifeactivity_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lifeactivity-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the book <i>Imperfect Garden: The Legacy of Humanism</i>, humanist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Tzvetan_Todorov" title="Tzvetan Todorov">Tzvetan Todorov</a> identifies individualism as an important current of socio-political thought within modernity and as examples of it he mentions <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Michel de Montaigne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_de_La_Rochefoucauld_(writer)" title="François de La Rochefoucauld (writer)">François de La Rochefoucauld</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Sade" title="Marquis de Sade">Marquis de Sade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Baudelaire" title="Charles Baudelaire">Charles Baudelaire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_University_Press_2002_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Press_2002-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In La Rochefoucauld, he identifies a tendency similar to <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">stoicism</a> in which "the honest person works his being in the manner of a sculptor who searches the liberation of the forms which are inside a block of marble, to extract the truth of that matter."<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_University_Press_2002_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Press_2002-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In Baudelaire he finds the <a href="/wiki/Dandy" title="Dandy">dandy</a> trait in which one searches to cultivate "the idea of beauty within oneself, of satisfying one's passions of feeling and thinking."<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_University_Press_2002_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Press_2002-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russian-American poet <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Brodsky" title="Joseph Brodsky">Joseph Brodsky</a> once wrote that "The surest defense against Evil is extreme individualism, originality of thinking, whimsicality, even—if you will—eccentricity. That is, something that can't be feigned, faked, imitated; something even a seasoned imposter couldn't be happy with."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a> famously declared, "Whoso would be a man must be a nonconformist"—a point of view developed at length in both the life and work of <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>. Equally memorable and influential on <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman" title="Walt Whitman">Walt Whitman</a> is Emerson's idea that "a foolish consistency is the hobgoblin of small minds, adored by little statesmen and philosophers and divines." Emerson opposes on principle the reliance on civil and religious social structures precisely because through them the individual approaches the divine second-hand, mediated by the once original experience of a genius from another age. "An institution," he explains, "is the lengthened shadow of one man." To achieve this original relation one must "Insist on one's self; never imitate" for if the relationship is secondary the connection is lost.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_individualism">Criticism of individualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Criticism of individualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some sociologists have criticized the excessive levels of individualism in the United States. T. W. Greene, for example, argued that three distinct yet overlapping ideologies of individualism are currently prevalent: (1) The ideology of self-willed wealth; (2) The ideology of full self-reliance; and (3) The ideology of high self-esteem. Each strand of individualism developed during periods of structural crisis and transition in the U.S. Individualistic ideologies sidetrack many Americans from acknowledging structural contributors to their nation's problems. The ideologies may cause adherents to believe all low-income individuals are lazy, for example, while dismissing evidence that the American middle class has declined for macro-economic reasons.  By causing persons to blame individuals solely for their misfortunes, the three individualistic ideologies perpetuate systems of inequality. The same ideologies can also fuel selfish behaviors.  Persons embracing individualistic ideologies may become preoccupied with attaining wealth, full self-reliance, and/or high self-esteem at the expense of altruism, healthy marriages, and the development of deep social bonds, which are assumed to be self-evident virtues.  Greene concluded that Americans are too individualistic in the "wrong" ways (preoccupied with attaining individual wealth, full self-reliance, and high self-esteem), while not being individualistic enough in the "right" ways (thinking critically and independently as individuals).<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> believes that the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord&#39;s Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a> opposes individualism because God loves everyone and everyone must be loved in turn.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<ul><li>Sundstrom, William A.  "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.scu.edu/ethics/publications/submitted/sundstrom/Sundstrommanifesto.pdf">An Egalitarian-Libertarian Manifesto</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20131029212045/http://www.scu.edu/ethics/publications/submitted/sundstrom/Sundstrommanifesto.pdf">Archived</a> October 29, 2013, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>."</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Individualism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The leading intellectual trait of the era was the recovery, to a certain degree, of the secular and humane philosophy of Greece and Rome. Another humanist trend which cannot be ignored was the rebirth of individualism, which, developed by Greece and Rome to a remarkable degree, had been suppressed by the rise of a caste system in the later Roman Empire, by the Church and by feudalism in the Middle Ages.<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.historyguide.org/intellect/humanism.html">"The history guide: Lectures on Modern European Intellectual History"</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Anthropocentricity and individualism...Humanism and Italian art were similar in giving paramount attention to human experience, both in its everyday immediacy and in its positive or negative extremes...The human-centredness of Renaissance art, moreover, was not just a generalized endorsement of earthly experience. Like the humanists, Italian artists stressed the autonomy and dignity of the individual."<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/275932/humanism/11824/Anthropocentricity-and-individualism">"Humanism" on Encyclopædia Britannica</a></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-thoreau-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-thoreau_55-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Paralelamente, al otro lado del atlántico, en el diferente contexto de una nación a medio hacer, los Estados Unidos, otros filósofos elaboraron un pensamiento individualista similar, aunque con sus propias especificidades. Henry David Thoreau (1817–1862), uno de los escritores próximos al movimiento de la filosofía trascendentalista, es uno de los más conocidos. Su obra más representativa es Walden, aparecida en 1854, aunque redactada entre 1845 y 1847, cuando Thoreau decide instalarse en el aislamiento de una cabaña en el bosque, y vivir en íntimo contacto con la naturaleza, en una vida de soledad y sobriedad. De esta experiencia, su filosofía trata de transmitirnos la idea que resulta necesario un retorno respetuoso a la naturaleza, y que la felicidad es sobre todo fruto de la riqueza interior y de la armonía de los individuos con el entorno natural. Muchos han visto en Thoreau a uno de los precursores del ecologismo y del anarquismo primitivista representado en la actualidad por Jonh Zerzan. Para George Woodcock, esta actitud puede estar también motivada por una cierta idea de resistencia al progreso y de rechazo al materialismo creciente que caracteriza la sociedad norteamericana de mediados de siglo XIX."<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.acracia.org/1-23a58lainsumision.pdf">"Voluntary non-submission. Spanish individualist anarchism during dictatorship and the second republic (1923–1938)"</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110723130358/http://www.acracia.org/1-23a58lainsumision.pdf">Archived</a> July 23, 2011, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Los anarco-individualistas, G.I.A...Una escisión de la FAI producida en el IX Congreso (Carrara, 1965) se pr odujo cuando un sector de anarquistas de tendencia humanista rechazan la interpretación que ellos juzgan disciplinaria del <i>pacto asociativo" clásico, y crean los GIA (Gruppi di Iniziativa Anarchica) . Esta pequeña federación de grupos, hoy nutrida sobre todo de veteranos anarco-individualistas de orientación pacifista, naturista, etcétera defiende la autonomía personal y rechaza a rajatabla toda forma de intervención en los procesos del sistema, como sería por ejemplo el sindicalismo. Su portavoz es L'Internazionale con sede en Ancona. La escisión de los GIA prefiguraba, en sentido contrario, el gran debate que pronto había de comenzar en el seno del movimiento"<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://eljorobado.enlucha.info/bicicleta/bicicleta/ciclo/01/17.htm">"El movimiento libertario en Italia" by </a></i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://eljorobado.enlucha.info/bicicleta/bicicleta/ciclo/01/17.htm">Bicicleta. REVISTA DE COMUNICACIONES LIBERTARIAS<i> Year 1 No. Noviembre, 1 1977</i></a><i> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20131012054206/http://eljorobado.enlucha.info/bicicleta/bicicleta/ciclo/01/17.htm">Archived</a> October 12, 2013, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-acracia.org-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-acracia.org_60-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Proliferarán así diversos grupos que practicarán el excursionismo, el naturismo, el nudismo, la emancipación sexual o el esperantismo, alrededor de asociaciones informales vinculadas de una manera o de otra al anarquismo. Precisamente las limitaciones a las asociaciones obreras impuestas desde la legislación especial de la Dictadura potenciarán indirectamente esta especie de asociacionismo informal en que confluirá el movimiento anarquista con esta heterogeneidad de prácticas y tendencias. Uno de los grupos más destacados, que será el impulsor de la revista individualista Ética será el Ateneo Naturista Ecléctico, con sede en Barcelona, con sus diferentes secciones la más destacada de las cuales será el grupo excursionista Sol y Vida."<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.acracia.org/1-23a58lainsumision.pdf">"La insumisión voluntaria: El anarquismo individualista español durante la Dictadura y la Segunda República (1923–1938)" by Xavier Díez</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110723130358/http://www.acracia.org/1-23a58lainsumision.pdf">Archived</a> July 23, 2011, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-aujourdhui-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-aujourdhui_61-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Les anarchistes individualistes du début du siècle l'avaient bien compris, et intégraient le naturisme dans leurs préoccupations. Il est vraiment dommage que ce discours se soit peu à peu effacé, d'antan plus que nous assistons, en ce moment, à un retour en force du puritanisme (conservateur par essence)."<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ytak.club.fr/natytak.html">"Anarchisme et naturisme, aujourd'hui." by Cathy Ytak</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090225212442/http://ytak.club.fr/natytak.html">Archived</a> February 25, 2009, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tucker said, <i>"the fact that one class of men are dependent for their living upon the sale of their labour, while another class of men are relieved of the necessity of labour by being legally privileged to sell something that is not labour. . . . And to such a state of things I am as much opposed as any one. But the minute you remove privilege. . . every man will be a labourer exchanging with fellow-labourers . . . What Anarchistic-Socialism aims to abolish is usury . . . it wants to deprive capital of its reward."</i>Benjamin Tucker. <i>Instead of a Book</i>, p. 404</span>

<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Michael Freeden identifies four broad types of individualist anarchism. He says the first is the type associated with William Godwin that advocates <a href="/wiki/Self-government" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-government">self-government</a> with a "progressive rationalism that included benevolence to others." The second type is the amoral self-serving rationality of <a href="/wiki/Egotism" title="Egotism">Egoism</a>, as most associated with Max Stirner. The third type is "found in <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a>'s early predictions, and in that of some of his disciples such as <a href="/wiki/Wordsworth_Donisthorpe" title="Wordsworth Donisthorpe">Donisthorpe</a>, foreseeing the redundancy of the state in the source of social evolution." The fourth type retains a moderated form of Egoism and accounts for social cooperation through the advocacy of market. <i>Freeden, Michael. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ocS-zA5zwSYC&amp;pg">Ideologies and Political Theory: A Conceptual Approach</a>. Oxford University Press. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-19-829414-X" title="Special:BookSources/0-19-829414-X">0-19-829414-X</a>. pp. 313–14</i>.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-123">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mendes, Silva. <i>Socialismo Libertário ou Anarchismo</i> Vol. 1 (1896): "Society should be free through mankind's spontaneous federative affiliation to life, based on the community of land and tools of the trade; meaning: Anarchy will be equality by abolition of <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a> (while retaining respect for <a href="/wiki/Personal_property" title="Personal property">personal property</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a> by abolition of <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a>".</span>

<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...what categorizes libertarian socialism is a focus on forms of social organization to further the freedom of the individual combined with an advocacy of non-state means for achieving this." <li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"What is implied by the term 'libertarian socialism'?: The idea that socialism is first and foremost about freedom and therefore about overcoming the domination, repression, and alienation that block the free flow of human creativity, thought, and action...An approach to socialism that incorporates cultural revolution, women's and children's liberation, and the critique and transformation of daily life, as well as the more traditional concerns of socialist politics. A politics that is completely revolutionary because it seeks to transform all of reality. We do not think that capturing the economy and the state lead automatically to the transformation of the rest of social being, nor do we equate liberation with changing our life-styles and our heads. Capitalism is a total system that invades all areas of life: socialism must be the overcoming of capitalist reality in its entirety, or it is nothing." "What is Libertarian Socialism?" by Ulli Diemer. Volume 2, Number 1 (Summer 1997 issue) of <i>The Red Menace</i>.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://chomsky.info/1986____/">"The Soviet Union Versus Socialism"</a>. <i>chomsky.info</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2015-11-22</span></span>. <q>Libertarian socialism, furthermore, does not limit its aims to democratic control by producers over production, but seeks to abolish all forms of domination and hierarchy in every aspect of social and personal life, an unending struggle, since progress in achieving a more just society will lead to new insight and understanding of forms of oppression that may be concealed in traditional practice and consciousness.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=chomsky.info&amp;rft.atitle=The+Soviet+Union+Versus+Socialism&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fchomsky.info%2F1986&#95;_&#95;_%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIndividualism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Authority is defined in terms of the right to exercise social control (as explored in the "sociology of power") and the correlative duty to obey (as explred in the "philosophy of practical reason"). Anarchism is distinguished, philosophically, by its scepticism towards such moral relations – by its questioning of the claims made for such normative power – and, practically, by its challenge to those "authoritative" powers which cannot justify their claims and which are therefore deemed illegitimate or without moral foundation."<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=kkj5i3CeGbQC&amp;printsec=frontcover#v=onepage"><i>Anarchism and Authority: A Philosophical Introduction to Classical Anarchism</i> by Paul McLaughlin. AshGate. 2007. p. 1</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ReferenceB-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceB_133-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Anarchism, then, really stands for the liberation of the human mind from the dominion of religion; the liberation of the human body from the dominion of property; liberation from the shackles and restraint of government. Anarchism stands for a social order based on the free grouping of individuals for the purpose of producing real social wealth; an order that will guarantee to every human being free access to the earth and full enjoyment of the necessities of life, according to individual desires, tastes, and inclinations." <a href="/wiki/Emma_Goldman" title="Emma Goldman">Emma Goldman</a>. "What it Really Stands for Anarchy" in <i><a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_Other_Essays" title="Anarchism and Other Essays">Anarchism and Other Essays</a></i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Individualist anarchist Benjamin Tucker defined anarchism as opposition to authority as follows "They found that they must turn either to the right or to the left, — follow either the path of Authority or the path of Liberty. Marx went one way; Warren and Proudhon the other. Thus were born State Socialism and Anarchism...Authority, takes many shapes, but, broadly speaking, her enemies divide themselves into three classes: first, those who abhor her both as a means and as an end of progress, opposing her openly, avowedly, sincerely, consistently, universally; second, those who profess to believe in her as a means of progress, but who accept her only so far as they think she will subserve their own selfish interests, denying her and her blessings to the rest of the world; third, those who distrust her as a means of progress, believing in her only as an end to be obtained by first trampling upon, violating, and outraging her. These three phases of opposition to Liberty are met in almost every sphere of thought and human activity. Good representatives of the first are seen in the Catholic Church and the Russian autocracy; of the second, in the Protestant Church and the Manchester school of politics and political economy; of the third, in the atheism of Gambetta and the socialism of Karl Marx." <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Tucker" title="Benjamin Tucker">Benjamin Tucker</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.theanarchistlibrary.org/HTML/Benjamin_Tucker__Individual_Liberty.html"><i>Individual Liberty.</i></a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Anarchist historian <a href="/wiki/George_Woodcock" title="George Woodcock">George Woodcock</a> report of <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a>'s anti-authoritarianism and shows opposition to both state and non-state forms of authority as follows: "All anarchists deny authority; many of them fight against it." (p. 9)...Bakunin did not convert the League's central committee to his full program, but he did persuade them to accept a remarkably radical recommendation to the Berne Congress of September 1868, demanding economic equality and implicitly attacking authority in both Church and State."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Brown_2002_106-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Brown_2002_106_136-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Brown, L. <li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It is forgotten that the early defenders of commercial society like <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">(Adam) Smith</a> were as much concerned with criticising the associational blocks to mobile labour represented by <a href="/wiki/Guilds" class="mw-redirect" title="Guilds">guilds</a> as they were to the activities of the state. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_socialism" title="History of socialism">history of socialist thought</a> includes a long associational and anti-statist tradition prior to the political victory of the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevism">Bolshevism</a> in the east and varieties of <a href="/wiki/Fabianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Fabianism">Fabianism</a> in the west. <li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In some ways, it is perhaps fair to say that if Left communism is an intellectual- political formation, it is so, first and foremost, negatively – as opposed to other socialist traditions. I have labelled this negative pole 'socialist orthodoxy', composed of both Leninists and social democrats...What I suggested was that these Left communist thinkers differentiated their own understandings of communism from a strand of socialism that came to follow a largely electoral road in the West, pursuing a kind of social capitalism, and a path to socialism that predominated in the peripheral and semi- peripheral countries, which sought revolutionary conquest of power and led to something like state capitalism. Generally, the Left communist thinkers were to find these paths locked within the horizons of capitalism (the law of value, money, private property, class, the state), and they were to characterize these solutions as statist, substitutionist and authoritarian." Chamsy el- Ojeili. <i>Beyond post-socialism. Dialogues with the far-left.</i> Palgrave Macmillan. 2015. pg 8</span>

<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Anarchist Peter Sabatini reports that In the United States "of early to mid-19th century, there appeared an array of communal and "utopian" counterculture groups (including the so-called <a href="/wiki/Free_love" title="Free love">free love</a> movement). <a href="/wiki/William_Godwin" title="William Godwin">William Godwin</a>'s anarchism exerted an ideological influence on some of this, but more so the socialism of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Owen" title="Robert Owen">Robert Owen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fourier" title="Charles Fourier">Charles Fourier</a>. One member of this commune was <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Warren" title="Josiah Warren">Josiah Warren</a> (1798–1874), considered to be the first <a href="/wiki/Individualist_anarchist" class="mw-redirect" title="Individualist anarchist">individualist anarchist</a>"<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.theanarchistlibrary.org/HTML/Peter_Sabatini__Libertarianism__Bogus_Anarchy.html">Peter Sabatini. <li id="cite_note-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-160">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The most ambitious contribution to literary anarchism during the 1890s was undoubtedly Oscar Wilde <i>The Soul of Man under Socialism</i>. Wilde, as we have seen, declared himself an anarchist on at least one occasion during the 1890s, and he greatly admired <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a>, whom he had met. Later, in <i>De Profundis</i>, he described Kropotkin's life as one "of the most perfect lives I have come across in my own experience" and talked of him as "a man with a soul of that beautiful white Christ that seems coming out of Russia." But in <i>The Soul of Man under Socialism</i>, which appeared in 1890, it is Godwin rather than Kropotkin whose influence seems dominant." George Woodcock. <i>Anarchism: A History of Libertarian Ideas and Movements.</i> 1962. (p. 447)</span>
